	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Thai Chinese</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">

<p><b>Thai Chinese</b> (also known as <b>Chinese Thais</b>, <b>Sino-Thais</b> or <b>Thais of Chinese origin</b>) are an ethnic <a href="/wiki/Chinese_ancestry" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese ancestry">Chinese</a> community in <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>. Thai Chinese are the largest minority group in Thailand and the largest <a href="/wiki/Overseas_Chinese" title="Overseas Chinese">overseas Chinese</a> community in the world with a population of approximately 10 million people, accounting for 14% of the total population of the country as of 2012. It is also the oldest and most prominent integrated overseas Chinese community. Slightly more than half of the ethnic Chinese population in Thailand trace their ancestry to eastern <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a>. This is evidenced by the prevalence of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Min" title="Southern Min">Southern Min</a> <a href="/wiki/Chaozhou_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Chaozhou dialect">Chaozhou dialect</a> among the Chinese in Thailand. A minority trace their ancestry to <a href="/wiki/Hakka_people" title="Hakka people">Hakka</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hainanese_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Hainanese people">Hainanese</a> immigrants.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thai Chinese are not a recognized minority group in Thailand, as Thai authorities consider them just a subgroup of the <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Thailand" title="Demographics of Thailand">Thai people</a>, akin to the <a href="/wiki/Thai_people" title="Thai people">Siamese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isan_people" title="Isan people">Isan people</a> or <a href="/wiki/Northern_Thai_people" title="Northern Thai people">Northern Thai</a> etc. The Thai Chinese have been deeply ingrained into all elements of Thai society over the past 200 years. The present Thai royal family, the <a href="/wiki/Chakri_dynasty" title="Chakri dynasty">Chakri dynasty</a>, was founded by King <a href="/wiki/Rama_I" title="Rama I">Rama I</a> who himself was partly Chinese. His predecessor, King <a href="/wiki/Taksin" title="Taksin">Taksin</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Thonburi_Kingdom" title="Thonburi Kingdom">Thonburi Kingdom</a>, was the son of a Thai mother and a Chinese immigrant from <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a> Province. With the highly successful integration of historic Chinese immigrant communities throughout Thailand, a significant number of Thai Chinese are the descendants of intermarriages between ethnic Chinese and native Thais. Many Thai Chinese have assimilated into Thai society and self-identify solely as Thai.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thai Chinese are a well established middle class ethnic group and are well represented in all levels of Thai society.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-google1_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google1-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-findarticles1_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-findarticles1-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> They play a leading role in Thailand's business sector and dominate the Thai economy today.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_179_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_179-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-google2_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google2-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In addition, Thai Chinese have a strong presence in Thailand's political scene with most of Thailand's former Prime Ministers and the majority of parliament having at least some Chinese ancestry.<sup id="cite_ref-Books.google.ca_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Books.google.ca-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Thais of Chinese descent are also well represented among Thailand's military and royalist elite.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thai_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Thailand has the largest overseas Chinese community in the world outside China.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Fourteen percent of Thailand's population is considered ethnic Chinese.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The Thai linguist <a href="/wiki/Theraphan_Luangthongkum" title="Theraphan Luangthongkum">Theraphan Luangthongkum</a> estimates the share of those having at least partial Chinese ancestry at about 40 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-Theraphan_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Theraphan-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Identity">Identity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thai_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Identity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>For assimilated second and third generation descendants of Chinese immigrants, it is principally a personal choice whether or not to identify themselves as ethnic Chinese.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Nonetheless, nearly all Thai Chinese sole self-identify as Thai, due to their close integration and successful assimilation into Thai society.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/G._William_Skinner" title="G. William Skinner">G. William Skinner</a> observed that the level of assimilation of the descendants of Chinese immigrants in Thailand disproved the "myth about the 'unchanging Chinese'", noting that "<i>assimilation is considered complete when the immigrant's descendant identifies himself in almost all social situations as a Thai, speaks Thai language habitually and with native fluency, and interacts by choice with Thai more often than with Chinese.</i>" <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESkinner1957237_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESkinner1957237-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Skinner believed that the assimilation success of the Thai Chinese was a result of the wise policy of the Thai rulers who, since the 17th century, allowed able Chinese tradesmen to advance their ranks into the kingdom's nobility.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESkinner1957240-41_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESkinner1957240-41-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The rapid and successful assimilation of the Thai Chinese has been celebrated by the Chinese descendants themselves, as evident in contemporary literature such as the novel <i>Letters from Thailand</i> (<a href="/wiki/Thai_language" title="Thai language">Thai</a>: <span lang="th">จดหมายจากเมืองไทย</span>) by Botan.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Today, the Thai Chinese constitute a significant part of the royalist/nationalist movements. When the then prime minister <a href="/wiki/Thaksin_Shinawatra" title="Thaksin Shinawatra">Thaksin Shinawatra</a>, who is Thai Chinese, was ousted from power in 2006, it was <a href="/wiki/Sondhi_Limthongkul" title="Sondhi Limthongkul">Sondhi Limthongkul</a>, another prominent Thai Chinese businessman, who formed and led People’s Alliance for Democracy (PAD) movement to protest the successive governments run by Thaksin's allies.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Mr. Sondhi accused Mr. Thaksin of corruption based on improper business ties between Thaksin's corporate empire and the Singapore-based <a href="/wiki/Temasek_Holdings" title="Temasek Holdings">Temasek Holdings Group</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The Thai Chinese in and around Bangkok were also the main participants of the months-long political campaign against the government of Ms. Yingluck (Mr. Thaksin's sister), between November 2013 and May 2014, the event which culminated in the military takeover in May 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thai_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a> traders, mostly from <a href="/wiki/Fujian_province" class="mw-redirect" title="Fujian province">Fujian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guangdong_province" class="mw-redirect" title="Guangdong province">Guangdong</a>, began arriving in <a href="/wiki/Ayutthaya_Kingdom" title="Ayutthaya Kingdom">Ayutthaya</a> by at least the 13th century. According to the <i>Chronicles of Ayutthaya</i>, <a href="/wiki/Sanpet_III" class="mw-redirect" title="Sanpet III">King Ekathotsarot</a> (r. 1605–1610) had been "concerned solely with ways of enriching his treasury," and was "greatly inclined toward strangers and foreign nations," especially <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>.
</p><p>Ayutthaya was under almost constant <a href="/wiki/Burmese%E2%80%93Siamese_wars" title="Burmese–Siamese wars">Burmese threat</a> from the 16th century onward, and the <a href="/wiki/Qianlong_Emperor" title="Qianlong Emperor">Qianlong Emperor</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a> was alarmed by Burmese military might. From 1766-1769, the Qianlong Emperor sent his armies four times to subdue the <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Burmese</a>, but the <a href="/wiki/Sino-Burmese_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Sino-Burmese War">Sino-Burmese Wars</a> ended in complete failure and Ayutthaya fell in the <a href="/wiki/Burmese%E2%80%93Siamese_War_(1765%E2%80%931767)" class="mw-redirect" title="Burmese–Siamese War (1765–1767)">Burmese–Siamese War (1765–1767)</a>. However, the Chinese efforts did divert the attention of Burma's Siam army. General <a href="/wiki/Taksin" title="Taksin">Taksin</a>, himself the son of a Chinese immigrant, took advantage of this to organize his force and attack the Burmese invaders. When he became king, Taksin actively encouraged Chinese immigration and trade. Settlers mainly from <a href="/wiki/Chaozhou" title="Chaozhou">Chaozhou</a> prefecture came to Siam in large numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Immigration continued over the following years, and the Chinese population in Thailand jumped from 230,000 in 1825 to 792,000 by 1910. By 1932, approximately 12.2 percent of the population of Thailand was Chinese.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The early Chinese immigration consisted almost entirely of men who did not bring women. Therefore, it became common for male Chinese immigrants to marry local Thai women. The children of such relationships were called <i>Sino-Thai</i><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> or <i>luk-jin</i> (ลูกจีน) in Thai.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> These Chinese-Thai intermarriages declined somewhat in the early 20th century, when significant numbers of Chinese women also began immigrating to Thailand.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Corruption" title="Corruption">corruption</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a> and the massive <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">population increase</a> in China, along with very high taxes, caused many men to leave China for Thailand in search of work. If successful, they sent money back to their families in China. Many Chinese immigrants prospered under the "<a href="/wiki/Tax_farming" class="mw-redirect" title="Tax farming">tax farming</a>" system, whereby private individuals were sold the right to collect taxes at a price below the value of the tax revenues.
</p><p>In the late 19th century, when Thailand was struggling to defend its independence from the colonial powers, Chinese bandits from Yunnan Province began making raids into the country in the <a href="/wiki/Haw_Wars" class="mw-redirect" title="Haw Wars">Haw Wars</a> (<a href="/wiki/Thai_language" title="Thai language">Thai</a>: <span lang="th">ปราบกบฏฮ่อ</span>). Thai nationalist attitudes at all levels were thus colored by anti-Chinese sentiment. Members of the Chinese community had long dominated domestic commerce and had served as agents for royal trade monopolies. With the rise of European economic influence, however, many Chinese shifted to opium trafficking and tax collecting, both of which were despised occupations.
</p><p>From 1882 to 1917, nearly 13,000 to 34,000 Chinese entered the country per year from <a href="/wiki/Southern_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Southern China">southern China</a> which was vulnerable to <a href="/wiki/Flood" title="Flood">floods</a> and <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">drought</a>, mostly settling in <a href="/wiki/Bangkok" title="Bangkok">Bangkok</a> and along the coast of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Siam" class="mw-redirect" title="Gulf of Siam">Gulf of Siam</a>. They predominated in occupations requiring arduous labor, skills, or entrepreneurship. They worked as blacksmiths, railroad laborers and <a href="/wiki/Rickshaw" title="Rickshaw">rickshaw</a> pullers. While most Thais were engaged in rice production, the Chinese brought new farming ideas and new methods to supply labor on its rubber plantations,  both domestically and internationally.<sup id="cite_ref-Sowell_1997_182–184_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sowell_1997_182–184-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">republican</a> ideas brought by the Chinese were considered seditious by the Thai government. For example, a translation of Chinese revolutionary <a href="/wiki/Sun_Yat-sen" title="Sun Yat-sen">Sun Yat-sen</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Three_Principles_of_the_People" title="Three Principles of the People">Three Principles of the People</a></i> was banned under the Communism Act of 1933. The government had regulated <a href="/wiki/Chinese_school" title="Chinese school">Chinese schools</a> even before <a href="/wiki/Compulsory_education" title="Compulsory education">compulsory education</a> was established in the country, starting with the Private Schools Act of 1918. This act required all foreign teachers to pass a <a href="/wiki/Thai_language" title="Thai language">Thai language</a> test and for principals of all schools to implement standards set by the Thai <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Education_(Thailand)" title="Ministry of Education (Thailand)">Ministry of Education</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-W_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Legislation by King <a href="/wiki/Vajiravudh" title="Vajiravudh">Rama VI (1910–1925)</a> that required the adoption of Thai surnames was largely directed at the Chinese community as a number of ethnic Chinese families left <a href="/wiki/Burma" class="mw-redirect" title="Burma">Burma</a> between 1930 and 1950 and settled in the <a href="/wiki/Ratchaburi_Province" title="Ratchaburi Province">Ratchaburi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kanchanaburi_Province" title="Kanchanaburi Province">Kanchanaburi Provinces</a> of western Thailand. A few of the ethnic Chinese families in that area had already emigrated from Burma in the 19th century.
</p><p>The Chinese in Thailand also suffered discrimination between the 1930s to 1950s under the military dictatorship of Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Plaek_Phibunsongkhram" title="Plaek Phibunsongkhram">Plaek Phibunsongkhram</a> (in spite of having part-Chinese ancestry himself),<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> which allied itself with the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Empire of Japan</a>. The Primary Education Act of 1932 made the <a href="/wiki/Thai_language" title="Thai language">Thai language</a> the compulsory medium of education, but as a result of protests from Thai Chinese, by 1939, students were allowed two hours per week of <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Mandarin</a> instruction.<sup id="cite_ref-W_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> State corporations took over commodities such as rice, tobacco, and petroleum and Chinese businesses found themselves subject to a range of new taxes and controls. By 1970, more than 90 percent of the Chinese born in <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> had abandoned Chinese citizenship and were granted Thai citizenship instead. In 1975, diplomatic relations were established with China.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thai_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Intermarriage with the <a href="/wiki/Thai_people" title="Thai people">Thais</a> has resulted in many people who claim Thai ethnicity with Chinese ancestry or mixed.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> People of Chinese descent are concentrated in the coastal areas of Thailand, principally <a href="/wiki/Bangkok" title="Bangkok">Bangkok</a> and Paknampho (<a href="/wiki/Nakhonsawan" class="mw-redirect" title="Nakhonsawan">Nakhonsawan</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Considerable segments of Thailand's economic, political and academic elite are of Chinese descent.<sup id="cite_ref-Theraphan_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Theraphan-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thai_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Today, nearly all ethnic Chinese in Thailand speak <a href="/wiki/Thai_language" title="Thai language">Thai</a> exclusively. Only elderly Chinese immigrants still speak their native <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">varieties of Chinese</a>. The fast and successful assimilation of Thai Chinese has been celebrated in contemporary literature such as "Letters from Thailand" (<a href="/wiki/Thai_language" title="Thai language">Thai</a>: <span lang="th">จดหมายจากเมืองไทย</span>) by a Thai Chinese author Botan.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
In the modern Thai language there are many signs of Chinese influence.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> In the 2000 census, 231,350 identified as speakers of a variant of Chinese (<a href="/wiki/Teochew_dialect" title="Teochew dialect">Teochew</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hokkien" title="Hokkien">Hokkien</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hainanese" title="Hainanese">Hainanese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Hakka_Chinese" title="Hakka Chinese">Hakka</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Theraphan_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Theraphan-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The Teochew dialect of Chinese has served as the language of Bangkok's influential Chinese merchants' circles since the foundation of the city in the 18th century. Today, businesses in <a href="/wiki/Yaowarat_Road" title="Yaowarat Road">Yaowarat Road</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charoen_Krung_Road" title="Charoen Krung Road">Charoen Krung Road</a> in Bangkok's <a href="/wiki/Samphanthawong_District" title="Samphanthawong District">Samphanthawong District</a> which constitute the city's "Chinatown" still feature bilingual signs in Chinese and Thai.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> A number of Chinese words have found their way into the Thai language, especially names of dishes and foodstuff, as well as basic numbers (such as those from "three" to "ten") and terms related to gambling.<sup id="cite_ref-Theraphan_2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Theraphan-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chin_Haw" title="Chin Haw">Chin Haw</a> Chinese speak <a href="/wiki/Southwestern_Mandarin" title="Southwestern Mandarin">Southwestern Mandarin</a>.
</p><p>Some Thai Chinese families encourage their children learn Mandarin Chinese to reap benefits from their ethnic Chinese identity as Mandarin has been increasingly the primary language of business for Overseas Chinese business communities.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_179_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_179-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> But most Thais who study Chinese do so because it boosts their business or career opportunity, rather than because of ethnic identity reason. Although Chinese language schools were closed during the nationalist period before and during the Second World War, the Thai government never tried to suppress the Chinese cultural expressions. Chinese owned businesses have adorned their shops with Chinese language signs, such as in the famous <a href="/wiki/Yaowarat" class="mw-redirect" title="Yaowarat">Yaowarat</a> commercial district. Chinese wuxia novels and movies inundated the Thai entertainment market between 1960s and 1980s. However, some Thai families are sending their children to newly established Mandarin language schools in hopes to take advantage of business opportunities in Mainland China.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_184–185_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_184–185-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The rise of China's global economic prominence has prompted many Thai Chinese business families to see Mandarin as a beneficial asset in partaking in economic links and conducting business between Thailand and Mainland China.<sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_184–185_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_184–185-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade_and_industry">Trade and industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thai_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Trade and industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Bamboo_network" title="Bamboo network">Bamboo network</a> and <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Thailand" title="Economy of Thailand">Economy of Thailand</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chinese_Thai_vendor.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Chinese_Thai_vendor.jpg/200px-Chinese_Thai_vendor.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Chinese_Thai_vendor.jpg/300px-Chinese_Thai_vendor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Chinese_Thai_vendor.jpg/400px-Chinese_Thai_vendor.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chinese_Thai_vendor.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thai Chinese in the past set up small enterprises such as street vending to eke out a living, a humble profession passed on to the present day.</div></div></div>
<p>Like much of Southeast Asia, Thai Chinese <a href="/wiki/Dominant_minority" title="Dominant minority">dominate</a> Thai commerce at every level of society.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_127_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_127-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_179_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_179-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Ethnic Chinese wield tremendous economic clout over their indigenous Thai majority counterparts and play a critical role in maintaining the country's economic vitality and prosperity.<sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_127_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_127-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_179_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_179-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> In Thailand, the economic power of the Chinese is far greater than that of their proportion in the population in addition to the Chinese being socioeconomically successful for hundreds of years than the indigenous host Thai population.<sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_179_14-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_179-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> With their powerful economic prominence, the Chinese virtually make up the country's entire economically wealthy elite.<sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_179_14-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_179-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The development policy spearheaded by the Thai government has provided many business opportunities for the ethnic Chinese. A distinct Sino-Thai business community has emerged as the dominant economic group, controlling virtually all the major business sectors across the country.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The modern Thai business sector is highly dependent on ethnic Chinese entrepreneurs and investors who control virtually all the country's banks and large conglomerates and their support is enhanced by the large presence of lawmakers and politicians whom are of at least part-Chinese themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_179_14-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_179-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The Thai Chinese, a disproportionate wealthy, market-dominant minority not only form a distinct ethnic community, they also form, by and large, an economically advantaged social class: the commercial middle and upper class in contrast to the poorer indigenous Thai majority working and underclass around them.<sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_179–183_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_179–183-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>British <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> agent <a href="/wiki/John_Crawfurd" title="John Crawfurd">John Crawfurd</a> used detailed company records kept on <a href="/wiki/Prince_of_Wales%27s_Island" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince of Wales&#39;s Island">Prince of Wales's Island</a> (present-day <a href="/wiki/Penang" title="Penang">Penang</a>) from 1815 to 1824 to report specifically on the economic aptitude of the 8,595 Chinese there as compared to others. He used the data to estimate the Chinese &#8212; about five-sixths of whom were unmarried men in the prime of life &#8212; "as equivalent to an ordinary population of above 37,000, and...to a numerical Malay population of more than 80,000!".<sup id="cite_ref-Crawfurd_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crawfurd-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>p.30</span></sup> He surmised this and other differences noted as providing, "a very just estimate of the comparative state of civilization among nations, or, which is the same thing, of the respective merits of their different social institutions."<sup id="cite_ref-Crawfurd_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crawfurd-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>p.34</span></sup> In 1879, the Chinese controlled all of the steam-powered rice mills, most of which were sold by the British. Most of the leading businessmen in Thailand were of Chinese extraction and accounted for a significant portion of the Thai upper class.<sup id="cite_ref-Sowell_1997_182–184_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sowell_1997_182–184-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> In 1890, despite British shipping domination in Bangkok, Chinese conducted 62 percent of the shipping sector, operating for agents for Western shipping firms as well as their own.<sup id="cite_ref-Sowell_1997_182–184_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sowell_1997_182–184-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> They also dominated the rubber industry, market gardening, sugar production, and fish exporting sectors. In Bangkok, Thai Chinese dominate the entertainment and media industries, being the pioneers of Thailand's early publishing houses, newspapers, and film studios.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Thai Chinese moneylenders wield considerable economic power over the poorer indigenous ethnic Thai peasants as accusations of bribery of government officials, wars between the <a href="/wiki/Hui_(secret_society)" title="Hui (secret society)">Chinese secret societies</a>, and use of violent tactics to collect taxes served to foster Thai resentment against the Chinese at a time when the community was expanding rapidly due to immigration. Chinese were also accused of producing poverty for the Thai peasant, charging astronomically high interest rates, when in reality, the Thai banking business was highly competitive.<sup id="cite_ref-Sowell_1997_182–184_38-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sowell_1997_182–184-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> In addition, Chinese millers and rice traders were blamed for an economic recession that gripped Siam for nearly a decade after 1905.<sup id="cite_ref-Sowell_1997_182–184_38-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sowell_1997_182–184-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Large waves of Han Chinese immigration occurred in the nineteenth and early in the twentieth century, peaking in the 1920s from southern China who were eager to make money and return to their families. By the end of the nineteenth century, the Chinese would lose their control of foreign trade to the European colonial powers and began to act as compradores for Western trading houses. Ethnic Chinese also entered extraction intensive industries such as tin mining, teak-cutting, sawmilling, rice-miling, as well as the transportation sector through building ports and railways.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MahaNakhon_by_kylehase.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/MahaNakhon_by_kylehase.jpg/320px-MahaNakhon_by_kylehase.jpg" decoding="async" width="320" height="443" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/MahaNakhon_by_kylehase.jpg/480px-MahaNakhon_by_kylehase.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/MahaNakhon_by_kylehase.jpg/640px-MahaNakhon_by_kylehase.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3623" data-file-height="5011" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MahaNakhon_by_kylehase.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bangkok" title="Bangkok">Bangkok</a> continues to be Thailand's major financial district and business networking hub for Thai Chinese businessmen.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/History_of_Thailand_(1932%E2%80%931973)" title="History of Thailand (1932–1973)">Mid-20th century Thailand</a> was isolationist with its economy mired in state-owned enterprises. 
The Chinese served as an important impetus for Thailand's modern industrialization rapidly transforming the Thai domestic economy into an export-oriented trade based economy linked with global capitalism.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Over the next several decades, internationalization and capitalist market-oriented policies led to the dramatic emergence of a massive export-oriented, large-scale manufacturing sector, which in turn catapulted Thailand into joining the <a href="/wiki/Tiger_Cub_Economies#Thailand" title="Tiger Cub Economies">Tiger Cub Economies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_35_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_35-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Virtually all the industrial manufacturing and import-export shipping firms establishments including the <a href="/wiki/Automotive_industry_in_Thailand" title="Automotive industry in Thailand">auto</a> manufacturing behemoth Siam Motors are Chinese controlled.<sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_35_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_35-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> In the years between <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> and <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Thailand's major exports, rice, tin, rubber, and timber were under Chinese control. Despite their small numbers as compared to the native Thai population, the Chinese have controlled virtually every line of business, ranging from small retail trade to large industries. Comprising merely ten percent of the population, ethnic Chinese dominate over four-fifths of the country's vital rice, tin, rubber, and timber exports, and virtually the country's entire wholesale and retail trade.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> By 1924, ethnic Chinese controlled three of the nine sawmills in Bangkok. Market gardening, sugar production (The Chinese introduced the sugar industry to Thailand), and fish exporting was dominated by the Chinese.<sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_179_14-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_179-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Virtually all of the new manufacturing establishments were Chinese controlled. Despite failed Thai <a href="/wiki/Affirmative_action" title="Affirmative action">affirmative action</a>-based policies in the 1930s to economically empower the impoverished indigenous Thai majority, 70 percent of retailing outlets and 80 to 90 percent of rice mills were controlled by ethnic Chinese.<sup id="cite_ref-Farron_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Farron-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> A survey of Thailand's roughly seventy most powerful business groups found that all but three were owned by Thai Chinese.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/Chinatown,_Bangkok" title="Chinatown, Bangkok">Bangkok has its own Chinatown</a>, Chinese influence is much more pervasive and subtle throughout the city. Bangkok's Thai Chinese clan associations are prominent throughout the city as the clans are major property holders and non-profit Chinese operated schools.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The Chinese control more than 80 percent of public companies listed on the Thai stock exchange.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kukrit_Pramoj" title="Kukrit Pramoj">Kukrit Pramoj</a>, the aristocratic former prime minister and distant relative of the Thai royal family, once said that most Thais had a Chinese relative "hanging somewhere on their family tree."<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> By the 1930s, the Thai Chinese minority dominated construction, industrial manufacturing, publishing, shipping, finance, commerce, and every industry in the country, minor, and major.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Minor industries included food vending, salt, tobacco, port, and bird's nest concessions.<sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_182_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_182-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Major industries included shipping, rice milling, tin manufacturing, rubber, teak, and petroleum.<sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_182_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_182-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, all the residential and commercial land in Central Siam were owned by Thai Chinese.<sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_182_81-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_182-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> 50 ethnic Chinese families controlled the country's entire business sectors equivalent to 81 to 90 percent of the overall market capitalization of the Thai economy with the remainder being either state owned and by a Thai Indian business family.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_35_70-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_35-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Highly publicized profiles of wealthy Chinese entrepreneurs attracted great public interest and were used to illustrate the community's strong economic clout.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> More than 80 percent of the top 40 richest people in Thailand are Thai of full or partial Chinese descent.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Thai Chinese entrepreneurs are influential in the real estate, <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Thailand" title="Agriculture in Thailand">agriculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Banking_in_Thailand" class="mw-redirect" title="Banking in Thailand">banking</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Finance_(Thailand)" title="Ministry of Finance (Thailand)">finance</a>, and the wholesale trading industries.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> In the 1990s, among the top ten Thai businesses in terms of sales, nine of them were Chinese-owned with only <a href="/wiki/Siam_Cement_Group" title="Siam Cement Group">Siam Cement</a> not being a Chinese-owned firm.<sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_179_14-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_179-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Of the five billionaires in Thailand in the late-20th century, all were all ethnic Chinese or of partial Chinese descent.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> On 17 March 2012, <a href="/wiki/Chaleo_Yoovidhya" title="Chaleo Yoovidhya">Chaleo Yoovidhya</a>, of humble Chinese origin, died while listed on <a href="/wiki/Forbes_list_of_billionaires" class="mw-redirect" title="Forbes list of billionaires">Forbes list of billionaires</a> as 205th in the world and 3rd in the nation, with an estimated net worth of US$5 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From an economic standpoint, Thai Chinese are seen as a fraction of the wealth they have created and added to the host country's economy, and representing what the Chinese have spent on themselves and their families.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> In the late 1950s, ethnic Chinese comprised 70 percent of Bangkok's business owners and senior business managers and 90 percent of the shares in Thai corporations are said to be held by Thais of Chinese extraction.<sup id="cite_ref-google1_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google1-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> 90 percent of Thailand's industrial and commercial capital are also held by ethnic Chinese.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> 90 percent of all investments in the industry and commercial sector and at least 50 percent of all investments in the banking and finance sectors is controlled by ethnic Chinese.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Economic advantages would also persist as Thai Chinese controlled 80 to 90 percent of the rice mills, the largest enterprises in the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Thailand’s lack of an indigenous Thai commercial culture in the private sector is dominated entirely by Thai Chinese themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Of the 25 leading entrepreneurs in the Thai business sector, 23 are ethnic Chinese or of partial Chinese descent.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Thai Chinese also comprise 96 percent of Thailand's 70 most powerful business groups with the remainder being the <a href="/wiki/TMB_Bank" title="TMB Bank">Thai Military Bank</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Crown_Property_Bureau" title="Crown Property Bureau">Crown Property Bureau</a>, and a Thai-Indian business group.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_35_70-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_35-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Family firms are extremely common in the Thai business sector as they are passed down from one generation to the next.<sup id="cite_ref-Yeung_2000_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yeung_2000-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> 90 percent of Thailand's manufacturing sector and 50 percent of Thailand's service sector is controlled by ethnic Chinese.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> According to a <i>Financial Statistics of the 500 Largest Public Companies in Asia Controlled by Overseas Chinese in 1994</i> chart released by Singaporean Geographer Dr. Henry Yeung of the <a href="/wiki/National_University_of_Singapore" title="National University of Singapore">National University of Singapore</a>, 39 companies were concentrated in Thailand with a market capitalization of US$35 billion and total assets of US$94 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-Yeung_2000_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yeung_2000-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> In Thailand, ethnic Chinese control the nations four largest private banks (Bangkok Bank, Thai Farmers Bank, Bank of Ahudya, and Bangkok Commercial Bank), of which <a href="/wiki/Bangkok_Bank" title="Bangkok Bank">Bangkok Bank</a> is the largest and most profitable private bank in addition to the Thai royal household being dependent on Chinese private equity capital and business expertise from these banks to fund and create more modern businesses.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Thai Chinese also dominate the <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_in_Thailand" title="Telecommunications in Thailand">Thai telecommunications sector</a> with well-known names such as the Shinawatra telecommunications group, Telcoms Asia, Jasmine, Ucom, and Samar.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Thai Chinese businesses are part of the larger <a href="/wiki/Bamboo_network" title="Bamboo network">bamboo network</a>, a network of <a href="/wiki/Overseas_Chinese" title="Overseas Chinese">Overseas Chinese</a> businesses operating in the markets of Southeast Asia that share common family, ethnic, language, and cultural ties.<sup id="cite_ref-Weidenbaum_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weidenbaum-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/1997_Asian_financial_crisis" title="1997 Asian financial crisis">1997 Asian financial crisis</a>, structural reforms imposed by the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a> (IMF) on Indonesia and Thailand led to the loss of many <a href="/wiki/Monopolistic_competition" title="Monopolistic competition">monopolistic positions</a> long held by the ethnic Chinese business elite.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Despite the financial and economic crisis, Thai Chinese are estimated to own 65 percent of the total banking assets, 60 percent of the national trade, 90 percent of all local investments in the commercial sector, 90 percent of all local investments in the manufacturing sector, and 50 percent of all local investments in the banking and financial services sector.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With the rise of <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_China" title="Economy of China">China</a> as a global economic power, Thai-Chinese businesses are now the largest investors in Mainland China among all overseas Chinese communities worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CP_Group_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CP_Group-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
The influx of Thai Chinese capital into Mainland China has led to a resurgence of Chinese cultural pride among the Thai Chinese community while concurrently pursuing new business opportunities while bringing their influx of foreign capital to create new jobs and economic niches on the Mainland. Many Thai Chinese have sent their children to newly established Chinese language schools, visiting China in record numbers, investing in China, and assuming Chinese surnames.<sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_184–185_50-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_184–185-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Charoen_Pokphand" title="Charoen Pokphand">Charoen Pokphand</a> (CP Group), a prominent Thai conglomerate claiming $9 billion in assets with US$25 billion in annual sales founded by the Thai-Chinese <a href="/wiki/Chearavanont" class="mw-redirect" title="Chearavanont">Chearavanont</a> family is one of the most powerful conglomerate companies investing in Mainland China today.<sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_41_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_41-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> It is currently the single largest foreign investor in China with over $1 billion USD invested with hundreds of businesses from agricultural food products, aquaculture, retail, leisure, industrial manufacturing and employing more than 150,000 people in China.<sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_179_14-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_179-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gomez_2012_94_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gomez_2012_94-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CP_Group_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CP_Group-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_41_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_41-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> It is known in China under well-known household names such as the "Chia Tai Group" and "Zheng Da Ji Tuan". CP Group also owns and operates <a href="/wiki/Tesco_Lotus" title="Tesco Lotus">Tesco Lotus</a>, one of the largest foreign hypermarket operators with 74 stores and seven distribution centers throughout 30 cities across China. One of CP Group's flagship businesses in China is a US$400 million Super Brand Mall, the largest mall in Shanghai's exclusive <a href="/wiki/Pudong" title="Pudong">Pudong</a> business district. Reignwood Pine Valley,<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> CP also controls Telecoms Asia, a joint venture with British Telecom since making its foray in the Thai telecommunications industry.<sup id="cite_ref-Gomez_2012_94_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gomez_2012_94-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> China's most exclusive golf and country clubs, were established and owned by a Thai-Chinese business tycoon, Chanchai Rouyrungruen (operator of <a href="/wiki/Red_Bull" title="Red Bull">Red Bull</a> drink business in China). It is cited as the most popular golf course in Asia. In 2008, Chanchai became the first owner of a business jet in Mainland China.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anand_Panyarachun#Education_and_career_in_civil_service_and_business" title="Anand Panyarachun">Anand's Saha-Union</a>, Thailand's leading industrial group, have so far invested over US$1.5 billion in China, and is operating more than 11 power plants in three of China's provinces. With over other 30 businesses in China, the company employs approximately 7,000 Chinese workers.<sup id="cite_ref-CP_Group_130-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CP_Group-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Central_Group" title="Central Group">Central Group</a>, Thailand's largest operator of shopping centers (and owner of Italy's leading department store, <a href="/wiki/La_Rinascente" title="La Rinascente">La Rinascente</a>) with US$3.5 billion in annual sales founded by a Thai-Chinese Chirathivat family, have recently opened three new large scale department stores in China.<sup id="cite_ref-CP_Group_130-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CP_Group-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As ethnic Chinese economic might grew, the indigenous Thai hill tribes and aborigines were gradually driven out into poorer land on the hills, on the rural outskirts of major Thai cities or into the mountains. The increased economic clout wielded by Thai Chinese has triggered distrust, resentment and Anti-Chinese sentiment among the poorer indigenous Thai majority, many whom engage in rural agrarian rice peasantry in a stark socioeconomic contrast to their modern, wealthier, and cosmopolitan middle class Chinese counterparts, who mainly engage in business, the skilled trades, or white collar professional occupations.<sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_179–183_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_179–183-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> During the early 20th-century, the Thai nationalist King <a href="/wiki/Vajiravudh" title="Vajiravudh">Vajiravudh</a>, also known as Rama VI wrote in his pamphlet <i>The Jews of the East</i> regarding his view of the Thai Chinese. Rama VI commented that the Thai Chinese were a "problem" for Thailand and compared the Thai Chinese to the <a href="/wiki/Jew" class="mw-redirect" title="Jew">Jews</a> as a group of outsider aliens who are loyal to their own ethnic group than that of their own host country.<sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_181–183_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_181–183-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> He conjuring up a scapegoating image of successful Chinese businessmen gaining their success at the expense of indigenous Thais resulting many Thai politicians to have tempted to blame Thai Chinese businessmen for Thailand's economic difficulties.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> King Vajiravudh's pamphlet was immensely influential among elite Thais and quickly spread to ordinary Thais, who were then filled with suspicion and hostility towards the Chinese minority.<sup id="cite_ref-Chua_2003_181–183_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chua_2003_181–183-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> The wealth disparity and abject poverty among the native ethnic Thai majority has resulted hostility blaming their extreme socioeconomic ills on the Chinese, especially Chinese moneylenders as "bloodsucking" exploitative debilitating <a href="/wiki/Shylock" title="Shylock">shylocks</a>. Beginning in the late 1930s and recommencing in the 1950s, the Thai government majority have dealt with this wealth disparity by pursuing a systematic and ruthless campaign of forced assimilation achieved through property confiscation, forced expropriation, coercive social policies, and draconian policies of anti-Chinese cultural suppression essentially destroying any trace of ethnic Han Chinese consciousness and identity.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Thai Chinese became targets of state discrimination that gave affirmative action privileges to the indigenous Thai majority peoples first while imposing reverse discrimination against the Chinese minority to gain a supposed equal economic footing.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Farron_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Farron-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thai_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Danfushen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Danfushen.jpg/200px-Danfushen.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Danfushen.jpg/300px-Danfushen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Danfushen.jpg/400px-Danfushen.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1229" data-file-height="922" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Danfushen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Chinese temple in <a href="/wiki/Bangkok" title="Bangkok">Bangkok</a></div></div></div>
<p>First-generation Chinese immigrants were followers of <a href="/wiki/Mahayana_Buddhism" class="mw-redirect" title="Mahayana Buddhism">Mahayana Buddhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a>. <a href="/wiki/Theravada_Buddhism" class="mw-redirect" title="Theravada Buddhism">Theravada Buddhism</a> has since become the religion of many ethnic Chinese in Thailand, especially among assimilated Chinese. Very often, many Chinese in Thailand combine practices of Chinese folk religion with Theravada Buddhism.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Major Chinese festivals such as <a href="/wiki/Chinese_New_Year" title="Chinese New Year">Chinese New Year</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mid-Autumn_Festival" title="Mid-Autumn Festival">Mid-Autumn Festival</a> and <a href="/wiki/Qingming_Festival" title="Qingming Festival">Qingming</a> are widely celebrated, especially in Bangkok, Phuket, and other parts of Thailand where there are large Chinese populations.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Peranakan" title="Peranakan">Peranakans</a> in <a href="/wiki/Phuket_city" class="mw-redirect" title="Phuket city">Phuket</a> are noted for their nine-day vegetarian festival between September and October. During the festive season, devotees will abstain from meat and <a href="/wiki/Mortification_of_the_flesh" title="Mortification of the flesh">mortification of the flesh</a> by Chinese mediums are also commonly seen, and the rites and rituals seen are devoted to the veneration of <a href="/wiki/Tua_Pek_Kong" title="Tua Pek Kong">Tua Pek Kong</a>. Such idiosyncratic traditions were developed during the 19th century in Phuket by the local Chinese with influences from Thai culture.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the north, there are some <a href="/wiki/Chinese_people" title="Chinese people">Chinese people</a> who practice <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>. They belong to a group of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_people" title="Chinese people">Chinese people</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/Chin_Haw" title="Chin Haw">Chin Ho</a>. Most of the Chinese Muslims are descended from <a href="/wiki/Hui_people" title="Hui people">Hui people</a> who live in <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan, China</a>. There are currently seven Chinese mosques in <a href="/wiki/Chiang_Mai" title="Chiang Mai">Chiang Mai</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> one of them is <a href="/wiki/Baan_Haw_Mosque" class="mw-redirect" title="Baan Haw Mosque">Baan Haw Mosque</a>, a well known mosque in the north.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialect_groups">Dialect groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thai_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Dialect groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The vast majority of Thai Chinese belong to various southern Chinese dialect groups. Of these, 56 percent are <a href="/wiki/Teochew_people" title="Teochew people">Teochew</a> (also commonly spelled as Teochiu), 16 percent <a href="/wiki/Hakka_people" title="Hakka people">Hakka</a> and 11 percent <a href="/wiki/Hainanese_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Hainanese people">Hainanese</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Cantonese_people" title="Cantonese people">Cantonese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hoklo_people" title="Hoklo people">Hokkien</a> each constitute seven percent of the Chinese population, and three percent belong to other Chinese dialect groups.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> A large number of Thai Chinese are the descendants of intermarriages between Chinese immigrants and Thais, while there are others who are of predominantly or solely of Chinese descent. People who are of mainly Chinese descent are descendants of immigrants who relocated to Thailand as well as other parts of <a href="/wiki/Nanyang_(geographical_region)" class="mw-redirect" title="Nanyang (geographical region)">Nanyang</a> (the Chinese term for Southeast Asia used at the time) in the early to mid-20th century due to famine and civil war in the southern Chinese provinces of <a href="/wiki/Hainan" title="Hainan">Hainan</a> (Hainanese), <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a> (Teochew, Cantonese and Hakka groups) and <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a> (Hokkien, Henghua, Hockchew and Hakka groups).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Teochew">Teochew</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thai_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Teochew">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Teochews mainly settled near the <a href="/wiki/Chao_Phraya_River" title="Chao Phraya River">Chao Phraya River</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bangkok" title="Bangkok">Bangkok</a>. Many of them worked in government, while others were involved in trade. During the reign of King Taksin, some influential Teochew traders were granted certain privileges. These prominent traders were called "royal Chinese" (<i>Jin-luang</i> or จีนหลวง in Thai).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hokkien">Hokkien</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thai_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Hokkien">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Hokkien community remains a dominant majority of Chinese living in <a href="/wiki/Trang_Province" title="Trang Province">Trang Province</a> and also <a href="/wiki/Phuket_Province" title="Phuket Province">Phuket Province</a> of Thailand.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hokchew_and_Peranakan">Hokchew and Peranakan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thai_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Hokchew and Peranakan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Phuket_Province" title="Phuket Province">Phuket Province</a>, the assimilated group is known as <a href="/wiki/Peranakan" title="Peranakan">Peranakans</a> or Phuket Baba. These people share a similar culture and identity with the <a href="/wiki/Peranakan" title="Peranakan">Peranakan Chinese</a> in neighboring <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, and Malaysia.
</p><p>The Hokchew is minor community in <a href="/wiki/Phuket_Province" title="Phuket Province">Phuket</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Some ethnic Chinese living in the Malay-dominated provinces in the far south use <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">Malay</a>, rather than Thai as a lingua franca, and many have intermarried with the local Malays.<sup id="cite_ref-Forbes_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>14-15</span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hakka">Hakka</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thai_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Hakka">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hakka_people" title="Hakka people">Hakkas</a> are mainly concentrated in <a href="/wiki/Chiang_Mai" title="Chiang Mai">Chiang Mai</a>, Phuket, and central western provinces. The Hakka own many private banks in Thailand, notably <a href="/wiki/Kasikorn_Bank" class="mw-redirect" title="Kasikorn Bank">Kasikorn Bank</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kiatnakin_Bank" title="Kiatnakin Bank">Kiatnakin Bank</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Linguistic_concentrations">Linguistic concentrations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thai_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Linguistic concentrations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>Teochew</li>
<li>Hokkien</li>
<li>Cantonese</li>
<li>Hakka</li>
<li>Hainanese</li>
<li>Hokchew</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Surnames">Surnames</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thai_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Surnames">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Almost all Sino-Thais, especially those who came to Thailand before the 1920s, possess a <a href="/wiki/Thai_surname" class="mw-redirect" title="Thai surname">Thai surname</a>, as was required by King <a href="/wiki/Rama_VI" class="mw-redirect" title="Rama VI">Rama VI</a> in order for them to become Thai citizens. The few who retain native <a href="/wiki/Chinese_surname" title="Chinese surname">Chinese surnames</a> are either recent immigrants or resident aliens.
</p><p>Sino-Thai surnames are often distinct from those of the general population, with generally longer names mimicking those of high officials and upper-class Thais<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> and with elements of these longer names retaining their original Chinese surname in translation or transliteration. For example, former Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Banharn_Silpa-Archa" class="mw-redirect" title="Banharn Silpa-Archa">Banharn Silpa-Archa</a>'s unusual <i>Archa</i> element is a translation into Thai of his family's former name <a href="/wiki/Ma_(surname)" title="Ma (surname)">Ma</a> (<small><a href="/wiki/Traditional_characters" class="mw-redirect" title="Traditional characters">trad.</a></small> 馬, <small><a href="/wiki/Simplified_characters" class="mw-redirect" title="Simplified characters">simp.</a></small> 马, <small>lit.</small> "horse"). Similarly, the <i>Lim</i> in <a href="/wiki/Sondhi_Limthongkul" title="Sondhi Limthongkul">Sondhi Limthongkul</a>'s name is the <a href="/wiki/Qiongwen" class="mw-redirect" title="Qiongwen">Hainanese</a> pronunciation of the name <a href="/wiki/Lin_(surname)" title="Lin (surname)">Lin</a> (林). Or it may have been done for them. For an example, see the <a href="/wiki/Abhisit_Vejjajiva#Background" title="Abhisit Vejjajiva">background of the Vejjajiva</a> Palace name.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Note that the latter-day <a href="/wiki/Royal_Thai_General_System_of_Transcription" title="Royal Thai General System of Transcription">Royal Thai General System of Transcription</a> would transcribe it as "Wetchachiwa" and that the Sanskrit-derived name refers to "medical profession."
</p><p>For immigrants who came between the 1920s and 1950s, it was common to simply prefix <i>Sae-</i> (from <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%A7%93" class="extiw" title="wikt:姓">姓</a>, "surname") to a transliteration of their name to form the new surname; <a href="/wiki/Wanlop_Saechio" title="Wanlop Saechio">Wanlop Saechio</a>'s last name thus derived from the Chinese <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%91%A8" class="extiw" title="wikt:周">周</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chanin_Sae-ear" title="Chanin Sae-ear">Chanin Sae-ear</a>'s last name <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%A5%8A" class="extiw" title="wikt:楊">楊</a>. Sae is also used by <a href="/wiki/Hmong_people" title="Hmong people">Hmong people</a> in Thailand.
</p><p>Many Thai Chinese people who immigrated after the 1950s use their Chinese surname, without Sae-.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prime_Ministers_of_Chinese_descent">Prime Ministers of Chinese descent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thai_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Prime Ministers of Chinese descent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

style="display:table-cell;vertical-align:middle;width:135px;height:135px;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto"><a href="/wiki/File:Banharn_Silpa-archa_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="Banharn Silpa-archa (cropped).jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Banharn_Silpa-archa_%28cropped%29.jpg/108px-Banharn_Silpa-archa_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="108" style="display:table-cell;vertical-align:middle;width:135px;height:135px;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto"><a href="/wiki/File:Yingluck_Shinawatra_at_US_Embassy,_Bangkok,_July_2011.jpg" decoding="async" width="101" height="135" data-file-width="573" data-file-height="766" /></a></div><div style="padding:1px"><a href="/wiki/Yingluck_Shinawatra" title="Yingluck Shinawatra">Yingluck Shinawatra</a></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div>
<p>Most of prime minister of Thailand are <a href="/wiki/Multiracial" title="Multiracial">multiracial</a>, however most of all have Chinese ancestry. The extent of Chinese heritage varies, in this case mean Chinese term are Teochew, Hoklo, Hainanese, Hakka and Cantonese pure bloodline or are majority only as follows (exclude <a href="/wiki/Chin_Haw" title="Chin Haw">Chin Haw</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peranakan" title="Peranakan">Peranakans</a>):
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Phraya_Manopakorn_Nititada" title="Phraya Manopakorn Nititada">Kon Hutasingha</a><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Phraya_Phahonphonphayuhasena" title="Phraya Phahonphonphayuhasena">Phot Phahonyothin</a><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Thawan_Thamrongnawasawat" title="Thawan Thamrongnawasawat">Thawan Thamrongnawasawat</a><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Pote_Sarasin" title="Pote Sarasin">Pote Sarasin</a><sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Thanin_Kraivichien" title="Thanin Kraivichien">Thanin Kraivichien</a><sup id="cite_ref-FEER_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FEER-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Chatichai_Choonhavan" title="Chatichai Choonhavan">Chatichai Choonhavan</a><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Chuan_Leekpai" title="Chuan Leekpai">Chuan Leekpai</a><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Banharn_Silpa-archa" title="Banharn Silpa-archa">Banharn Silpa-archa</a><sup id="cite_ref-bp_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bp-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Thaksin_Shinawatra" title="Thaksin Shinawatra">Thaksin Shinawatra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yingluck_Shinawatra" title="Yingluck Shinawatra">Yingluck Shinawatra</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BP_09_11_29_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BP_09_11_29-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>The Thai prime minister who <i>lacked</i> recent Chinese ancestry were <a href="/wiki/Plaek_Phibunsongkhram" title="Plaek Phibunsongkhram">Plaek Phibunsongkhram</a><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Seni_Pramoj" title="Seni Pramoj">Seni Pramoj</a><sup id="cite_ref-SeniKukrit_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SeniKukrit-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Pridi_Banomyong" title="Pridi Banomyong">Pridi Banomyong</a><sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-泰国_洪林,_黎道纲主编_2006_17_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-泰国_洪林,_黎道纲主编_2006_17-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Thanom_Kittikachorn" title="Thanom Kittikachorn">Thanom Kittikachorn</a>, <sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Kukrit_Pramoj" title="Kukrit Pramoj">Kukrit Pramoj</a><sup id="cite_ref-SeniKukrit_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SeniKukrit-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Suchinda_Kraprayoon" title="Suchinda Kraprayoon">Suchinda Kraprayoon</a><sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Chavalit_Yongchaiyudh" title="Chavalit Yongchaiyudh">Chavalit Yongchaiyudh</a><sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Surayud_Chulanont" title="Surayud Chulanont">Surayud Chulanont</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samak_Sundaravej" title="Samak Sundaravej">Samak Sundaravej</a><sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Abhisit_Vejjajiva" title="Abhisit Vejjajiva">Abhisit Vejjajiva</a><sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>. Also, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Prayut_Chan-o-cha" title="Prayut Chan-o-cha">Prayut Chan-o-cha</a> does not have any Chinese ancestry, with all of his ancestry come from <a href="/wiki/Nakhon_Ratchasima_Province" title="Nakhon Ratchasima Province">Nakhon Ratchasima Province</a> and he is pure <a href="/wiki/Thai_people" title="Thai people">Siamese</a><sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup>.
</p>

Bentley. p.&#160;30. <a href="/wiki/OCLC" title="OCLC">OCLC</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/oclc/03452414">03452414</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">February 2,</span> 2012</span>. <q>The Chinese amount to 8595, and are landowtiers, field-labourers, mechanics of almost every description, shopkeepers, and general merchants. They are all from the two provinces of Canton and Fo-kien, and threefourths of them from the latter. About five-sixths of the whole number are unmarried men, in the prime of life&#160;: so that, in fact, the Chinese population, in point of effective labour, may be estimated as equivalent to an ordinary population of above 37,000, and, as will afterwards be shown, to a numerical Malay population of more than 80,000!</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Chapter+I&amp;rft.btitle=Journal+of+an+Embassy+from+the+Governor-general+of+India+to+the+Courts+of+Siam+and+Cochin+China&amp;rft.place=London&amp;rft.pages=30&amp;rft.edition=2nd&amp;rft.pub=H.+Colburn+and+R.+Bentley&amp;rft.date=2006-08-21&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F03452414&amp;rft.aulast=Crawfurd&amp;rft.aufirst=John&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2F%3Fid%3DsAUPAAAAYAAJ%26q%3Dequivalent%2Bto%2Ban%2Bordinary%2Bpopulation%2Bof%2Babove%2B37%252C000%252C%23v%3Dsnippet%26q%3Dequivalent%2520to%2520an%2520ordinary%2520population%2520of%2520above%252037%252C000%252C%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AThai+Chinese" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

Also, the Université Paris (1 PanthéonSorbonne) in 2000 celebrated the centenary of Pridi and honored him as "one of the great constitutionalists of the twentieth century," comparing him to such figures as Rousseau, Montesquieu, and de Tocqueville.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Research+%26+Articles+on+Pridi+Banomyong&amp;rft.pub=BookRags&amp;rft.date=2012-06-18&amp;rft.au=Gerald+W.+Fry&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.bookrags.com%2Fresearch%2Fpridi-banomyong-ema-05%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AThai+Chinese" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
